eug-14-4OK, so debator B has answered my question about whether modifications to the Lambda-CDM model parameters could make the galaxies fit the model - unsurprisingly changing the assumptions about how stars mass and luminosity are related would work. They also point out that the redshifts for these galaxies were estimated using photometry, not spectroscopy. It's pretty common for lower redshift galaxies with a lot of hot dust (usually from very active star formation) to get confused for higher redshift galaxies when you only have a photometric red shift. These only get a correct red shift when spectroscopy is used as a comparison point. Debater A saying "While spectroscopy would give us more accurate measurements of redshifts, the new values will not vary too much from the original photometric estimate." when we know this is something that can and does happen quite often, especially for very high Z galxies is pretty bogus.

The other argument is how much the changes each debater is proposing to explain the galaxies will affect the rest of physics. I'm with debator B that whilst changing assumptions about stellar luminosity to mass relations isn't trivial, it is also predicted by pretty robust theory, despite the fact that we can't measure metallicity of the galaxies directly. We can measure the mass/luminosity relationship of low metallicity stars and show that they are more massive. Throwing out dark matter breaks some really fundamental things: gravity, gravitational lensing, galactic rotation curves etc. We've not yet observed dark matter directly (and it is a bit embarrassing that we essentially don't know what 90% of the universe is made of) but we see its effects everywhere. It would be a bit like arguing that mass didn't exist because we hadn't observed the Higgs boson.
